<p>
The <b>Kyoto Protocol</b> is an international <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> which extends the 1992 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) that commits state parties to reduce <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions, based on the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">scientific consensus</a> that (part one) <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> is occurring and (part two) it is extremely likely that human-made <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">CO<sub>2</sub> emissions</a> have predominantly caused it. The Kyoto Protocol was adopted in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> on 11 December 1997 and entered into force on 16 February 2005. There are currently 192 parties (<a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> withdrew from the protocol, effective December 2012)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNlist_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNlist-4">[4]</a></sup> to the Protocol.
</p><p>The Kyoto Protocol implemented the objective of the UNFCCC to reduce the onset of global warming by reducing greenhouse gas concentrations in the atmosphere to "a level that would prevent dangerous anthropogenic interference with the climate system" (Article 2). The Kyoto Protocol applies to the six greenhouse gases listed in Annex A: Carbon dioxide (CO2), Methane (CH4), Nitrous oxide (N2O), Hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs) Perfluorocarbons (PFCs) and Sulphur hexafluoride (SF6). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Protocol is based on the principle of common but differentiated responsibilities: it acknowledges that individual countries have different capabilities in combating climate change, owing to <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>, and ergo puts the obligation to reduce current emissions on developed countries on the basis that they are historically responsible for the current levels of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere.
</p><p>The Protocol's first commitment period started in 2008 and ended in 2012. A second commitment period was agreed on in 2012, known as the Doha Amendment to the Kyoto Protocol, in which 37 countries have binding targets: <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (and its 28 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_European_Union" title="Member states of the European Union">member states</a>), <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. Belarus, Kazakhstan and Ukraine have stated that they may withdraw from the Kyoto Protocol or not put into legal force the Amendment with second round targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-figueres_doha_summary_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-figueres_doha_summary-7">[7]</a></sup> Japan, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> have participated in Kyoto's first-round but have not taken on new targets in the second commitment period. Other developed countries without second-round targets are Canada (which withdrew from the Kyoto Protocol in 2012) and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (which has not ratified). As of July 2016, 66<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> states have accepted the Doha Amendment, while entry into force requires the acceptances of 144 states. Of the 37 countries with binding commitments, 7 have ratified.
</p><p>Negotiations were held in the framework of the yearly UNFCCC Climate Change Conferences on measures to be taken after the second commitment period ends in 2020. This resulted in the 2015 adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a>, which is a separate instrument under the UNFCCC rather than an amendment of the Kyoto Protocol.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" title="Global climate model">Global climate model</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">Scientific opinion on climate change</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kyoto_Parties_with_first_period_(2008-2012)_greenhouse_gas_emissions_limitations_targets_and_the_percentage_change_in_their_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990_and_2009.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="894" height="376" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Kyoto_Parties_with_first_period_%282008-2012%29_greenhouse_gas_emissions_limitations_targets_and_the_percentage_change_in_their_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990_and_2009.png/400px-thumbnail.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Kyoto_Parties_with_first_period_%282008-2012%29_greenhouse_gas_emissions_limitations_targets_and_the_percentage_change_in_their_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990_and_2009.png/600px-thumbnail.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Kyoto_Parties_with_first_period_%282008-2012%29_greenhouse_gas_emissions_limitations_targets_and_the_percentage_change_in_their_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990_and_2009.png/800px-thumbnail.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kyoto_Parties_with_first_period_(2008-2012)_greenhouse_gas_emissions_limitations_targets_and_the_percentage_change_in_their_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990_and_2009.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kyoto Parties with first period (2008–12) greenhouse gas emissions limitations targets, and the percentage change in their carbon dioxide emissions from fuel combustion between 1990 and 2009. For more detailed country/region information, see <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">Kyoto Protocol and government action</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Overview_map_of_states_committed_to_greenhouse_gas_limitations_in_the_first_Kyoto_Protocol_period_%28years_2008-2012%29_%28greyscale%29.png/600px-Overview_map_of_states_committed_to_greenhouse_gas_limitations_in_the_first_Kyoto_Protocol_period_%28years_2008-2012%29_%28greyscale%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Overview_map_of_states_committed_to_greenhouse_gas_limitations_in_the_first_Kyoto_Protocol_period_%28years_2008-2012%29_%28greyscale%29.png/800px-Overview_map_of_states_committed_to_greenhouse_gas_limitations_in_the_first_Kyoto_Protocol_period_%28years_2008-2012%29_%28greyscale%29.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Overview_map_of_states_committed_to_greenhouse_gas_limitations_in_the_first_Kyoto_Protocol_period_(years_2008-2012)_(greyscale).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of states committed to greenhouse gas (GHG) limitations in the first Kyoto Protocol period (2008–12):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><br/> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #000000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Annex I Parties who have agreed to reduce their GHG emissions below their individual base year levels (see definition in this article)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #737373; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Annex I Parties who have agreed to cap their GHG emissions at their base year levels</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f2f2f2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Non-Annex I Parties who are not obligated by caps or Annex I Parties with an emissions cap that allows their emissions to expand above their base year levels or countries that have not ratified the Kyoto Protocol</div> <br/> For specific emission reduction commitments of Annex I Parties, see the section of the article on <a href='/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#2012_emission_targets_and_"flexible_mechanisms"' title="Kyoto Protocol">2012 emission targets and "flexible mechanisms"</a>.<br/> <br/> The European Union as a whole has in accordance with this treaty committed itself to a 6.7% reduction. However, many member states (such as Greece, Spain, Ireland and Sweden) have not committed themselves to any reduction while France has committed itself not to expand its emissions (0% reduction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The view that human activities are likely responsible for most of the observed increase in global mean temperature ("global warming") since the mid-20th century is an accurate reflection of current scientific thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_us_nrc_Understanding_and_Responding_to_Climate_Change_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-2008_us_nrc_Understanding_and_Responding_to_Climate_Change-12">[12]</a></sup> Human-induced warming of the climate is expected to continue throughout the 21st century and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_us_nrc_Understanding_and_Responding_to_Climate_Change_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-2008_us_nrc_Understanding_and_Responding_to_Climate_Change-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC, 2007) have produced a range of projections of what the future increase in global mean temperature might be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_ipcc_global_warming_projections_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007_ipcc_global_warming_projections-13">[13]</a></sup>  The IPCC's projections are <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Baselines" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">"baseline" projections</a>, meaning that they assume no future efforts are made to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. The IPCC projections cover the time period from the beginning of the 21st century to the end of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_ipcc_global_warming_projections_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-2007_ipcc_global_warming_projections-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>  The "likely" range (as assessed to have a greater than 66% probability of being correct, based on the IPCC's expert judgement) is a projected increase in global mean temperature over the 21st century of between 1.1 and 6.4 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_ipcc_global_warming_projections_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-2007_ipcc_global_warming_projections-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The range in temperature projections partly reflects different projections of future greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_karl_ipcc_global_warming_projections_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-2009_karl_ipcc_global_warming_projections-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>22–24</span></sup>  Different projections contain different assumptions of future social and economic development (<a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Population_projection" title="Population projection">population level</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy" title="Energy policy">energy policies</a>), which in turn affects projections of future greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_karl_ipcc_global_warming_projections_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-2009_karl_ipcc_global_warming_projections-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>22–24</span></sup> The range also reflects uncertainty in the response of the climate system to past and future GHG emissions (measured by the <a href="/wiki/Climate_sensitivity" title="Climate sensitivity">climate sensitivity</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_karl_ipcc_global_warming_projections_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-2009_karl_ipcc_global_warming_projections-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>22–24</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chronology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_climate_change_science" title="History of climate change science">History of climate change science</a></div>
<p><b>1992</b> The UN Conference on the Environment and Development is held in Rio de Janeiro. It results in the Framework Convention on Climate Change <a class="external text" href="http://www.unfccc.int/" rel="nofollow">("FCCC" or "UNFCCC")</a> among other agreements.
</p><p><b>1995</b> Parties to the UNFCCC meet in Berlin (the 1st Conference of Parties (COP) to the UNFCCC) to outline specific targets on emissions.
</p><p><b>1997</b> In December the parties conclude the Kyoto Protocol in Kyoto, Japan, in which they agree to the broad outlines of emissions targets.
</p><p><b>2004</b> Russia and Canada ratify the Kyoto Protocol to the UNFCCC bringing the treaty into effect on February 16, 2005.
</p><p><b>2011</b> Canada became the first signatory to announce its withdrawal from the Kyoto Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>2012</b> On December 31, 2012, the first commitment period under the Protocol expired.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Article_2_of_the_UNFCCC">Article 2 of the UNFCCC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Article 2 of the UNFCCC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change#Interpreting_Article_2" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change § Interpreting Article 2</a></div>
<p>Most countries are Parties to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>  Article 2 of the Convention states its ultimate objective, which is to stabilize the concentration of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere "at a level that would prevent dangerous anthropogenic (human) interference with the climate system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_sciences" title="Natural sciences">natural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">technical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> can provide information on decisions relating to this objective including the possible magnitude and rate of future climate changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC-18">[18]</a></sup> However, the IPCC has also concluded that the decision of what constitutes "dangerous" interference requires value judgements, which will vary between different regions of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC-18">[18]</a></sup> Factors that might affect this decision include the local consequences of climate change impacts, the ability of a particular region to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming" title="Adaptation to global warming">adapt to climate change</a> (adaptive capacity), and the ability of a region to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">reduce its GHG emissions</a> (mitigative capacity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png"><img alt="Atmospheric greenhouse gas concentrations" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="1012" height="134" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/400px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Kyoto is intended to cut <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">global</a> emissions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_(vertical).png"><img alt="Refer to caption" data-file-height="934" data-file-width="703" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29.png/200px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29.png/300px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29.png //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29.png/400px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">In order to stabilize the atmospheric concentration of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, emissions worldwide would need to be dramatically reduced from their present level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>The main goal of the Kyoto Protocol is to control emissions of the main anthropogenic (human-emitted) greenhouse gases (GHGs) in ways that reflect underlying national differences in GHG emissions, wealth, and capacity to make the reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2004_grubb_kyoto_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-2004_grubb_kyoto-20">[20]</a></sup>  The treaty follows the main principles agreed in the original 1992 UN Framework Convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2004_grubb_kyoto_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-2004_grubb_kyoto-20">[20]</a></sup>  According to the treaty, in 2012, Annex I Parties who have ratified the treaty must have fulfilled their obligations of greenhouse gas emissions limitations established for the Kyoto Protocol's first commitment period (2008–2012). These emissions limitation commitments are listed in Annex B of the Protocol.
</p><p>The Kyoto Protocol's first round commitments are the first detailed step taken within the UN Framework Convention on Climate Change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-21">[21]</a></sup> The Protocol establishes a structure of rolling emission reduction commitment periods. It set a timetable starting in 2006 for negotiations to establish emission reduction commitments for a second commitment period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_commitments_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_commitments-22">[22]</a></sup> The first period emission reduction commitments expired on December 31, 2012.
</p><p>The ultimate objective of the UNFCCC is the "stabilization of greenhouse gas concentrations in the atmosphere at a level that would stop dangerous anthropogenic interference with the climate system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc2005_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc2005-23">[23]</a></sup> Even if Annex I Parties succeed in meeting their first-round commitments, much greater emission reductions will be required in future to stabilize atmospheric GHG concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_commitments_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_commitments-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_kyoto_stabilization_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_kyoto_stabilization-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>For each of the different anthropogenic GHGs, different levels of emissions reductions would be required to meet the objective of stabilizing atmospheric concentrations (see <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change#Stabilization_of_greenhouse_gas_concentrations" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change#Stabilization of greenhouse gas concentrations</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_meehl_stabilizing_atmospheric_concentrations_of_GHGs_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007_meehl_stabilizing_atmospheric_concentrations_of_GHGs-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) is the most important anthropogenic GHG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>  Stabilizing the concentration of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in the atmosphere would ultimately require the effective elimination of anthropogenic <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007_meehl_stabilizing_atmospheric_concentrations_of_GHGs_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-2007_meehl_stabilizing_atmospheric_concentrations_of_GHGs-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the principal concepts of the Kyoto Protocol are:
</p>
<ul><li>Binding commitments for the Annex I Parties. The main feature of the Protocol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_overview_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_overview-27">[27]</a></sup>  is that it established legally binding commitments to reduce emissions of greenhouse gases for Annex I Parties.  The commitments were based on the Berlin Mandate, which was a part of UNFCCC negotiations leading up to the Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-liverman-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>290</span></sup></li>
<li>Implementation. In order to meet the objectives of the Protocol, Annex I Parties are required to prepare policies and measures for the reduction of greenhouse gases in their respective countries. In addition, they are required to increase the absorption of these gases and utilize all mechanisms available, such as joint implementation, the clean development mechanism and emissions trading, in order to be rewarded with credits that would allow more greenhouse gas emissions at home.</li>
<li>Minimizing Impacts on Developing Countries by establishing an <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation" title="Climate change adaptation">adaptation</a> fund for climate change.</li>
<li>Accounting, Reporting and Review in order to ensure the integrity of the Protocol.</li>
<li>Compliance. Establishing a Compliance Committee to enforce compliance with the commitments under the Protocol.</li></ul>
<h2><span id="First_commitment_period:_2008.E2.80.932012"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_commitment_period:_2008–2012">First commitment period: 2008–2012</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First commitment period: 2008–2012">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Under the Kyoto Protocol, 37 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialized_countries" title="Industrialized countries">industrialized countries</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Community" title="European Community">European Community</a> (the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>-15, made up of 15 states at the time of the Kyoto negotiations) commit themselves to binding targets for GHG emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_overview_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_overview-27">[27]</a></sup>  The targets apply to the four greenhouse gases <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur_hexafluoride" title="Sulphur hexafluoride">sulphur hexafluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>), and two groups of gases, <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">hydrofluorocarbons</a> (HFCs) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfluorocarbon" title="Perfluorocarbon">perfluorocarbons</a> (PFCs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_gases_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_gases-30">[30]</a></sup> The six GHG are translated into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co2e" title="Co2e">CO<sub>2</sub> equivalents</a> in determining reductions in emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> These reduction targets are in addition to the industrial gases, chlorofluorocarbons, or CFCs, which are dealt with under the 1987 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol_on_Substances_that_Deplete_the_Ozone_Layer" title="Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer">Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer</a>.
</p><p>Under the Protocol, only the Annex I Parties have committed themselves to national or joint reduction targets (formally called "quantified emission limitation and reduction objectives" (QELRO) – Article 4.1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc1997_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc1997-32">[32]</a></sup>  Parties to the Kyoto Protocol not listed in Annex I of the Convention (the non-Annex I Parties) are mostly low-income developing countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup>  and may participate in the Kyoto Protocol through the Clean Development Mechanism (explained below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_commitments_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_commitments-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emissions limitations of Annex I Parties varies between different Parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_targets_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_targets-34">[34]</a></sup>  Some Parties have emissions limitations reduce below the base year level, some have limitations at the base year level (no permitted increase above the base year level), while others have limitations above the base year level.
</p><p>Emission limits do not include emissions by international aviation and shipping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shippingandaviation_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-shippingandaviation-35">[35]</a></sup> Although Belarus and Turkey are listed in the Convention's Annex I, they do not have emissions targets as they were not Annex I Parties when the Protocol was adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_targets_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_targets-34">[34]</a></sup>  Kazakhstan does not have a target, but has declared that it wishes to become an Annex I Party to the Convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_targets_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_targets-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:left;;">Annex I countries under the Kyoto Protocol, their 2008–2012 commitments (% of base year) and 1990 emission levels (% of all Annex I countries)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">

</div></div>
<p>For most state parties, 1990 is the base year for the national GHG inventory and the calculation of the assigned amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_reference_manual_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-2008_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_reference_manual-38">[38]</a></sup> However, five state parties have an alternative base year:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_reference_manual_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-2008_unfccc_kyoto_protocol_reference_manual-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Bulgaria: 1988;</li>
<li>Hungary: the average of the years 1985–1987;</li>
<li>Poland: 1988;</li>
<li>Romania: 1989;</li>
<li>Slovenia: 1986.</li></ul>
<p>Annex I Parties can use a range of sophisticated "flexibility" mechanisms (see below) to meet their targets. Annex I Parties can achieve their targets by allocating reduced annual allowances to major operators within their borders, or by allowing these operators to exceed their allocations by offsetting any excess through a mechanism that is agreed by all the parties to the UNFCCC, such as by buying <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emission allowances</a> from other operators which have excess emissions credits.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flexibility_mechanisms">Flexibility mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Flexibility mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Kyoto_Protocol" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">Economics of climate change mitigation § Kyoto Protocol</a></div>
<p>The Protocol defines three "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flexibility_mechanisms" title="Flexibility mechanisms">flexibility mechanisms</a>" that can be used by Annex I Parties in meeting their emission limitation commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>402</span></sup>  The flexibility mechanisms are International Emissions Trading (IET), the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> (CDM), and <a href="/wiki/Joint_Implementation" title="Joint Implementation">Joint Implementation</a> (JI). IET allows Annex I Parties to "trade" their emissions (<a href="/wiki/Assigned_amount_units" title="Assigned amount units">Assigned Amount Units</a>, AAUs, or "allowances" for short).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The economic basis for providing this flexibility is that the <a href="/wiki/Margin_(economics)" title="Margin (economics)">marginal</a> cost of reducing (or abating) emissions differs among countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toth_2001_flexibility_mechanisms_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-toth_2001_flexibility_mechanisms-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>660</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> "Marginal cost" is the cost of abating the last tonne of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>-eq for an Annex I/non-Annex I Party. At the time of the original Kyoto targets, studies suggested that  the flexibility mechanisms could reduce the overall (<a href="/wiki/Aggregate_data" title="Aggregate data">aggregate</a>) cost of meeting the targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hourcade_2001_economic_costs_of_flexibility_mechanisms_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-hourcade_2001_economic_costs_of_flexibility_mechanisms-43">[43]</a></sup>  Studies also showed that national losses in Annex I <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) could be reduced by use of the flexibility mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hourcade_2001_economic_costs_of_flexibility_mechanisms_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-hourcade_2001_economic_costs_of_flexibility_mechanisms-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The CDM and JI are called "project-based mechanisms," in that they generate emission reductions from projects. The difference between IET and the project-based mechanisms is that IET is based on the setting of a quantitative restriction of emissions, while the CDM and JI are based on the idea of "production" of emission reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toth_2001_flexibility_mechanisms_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-toth_2001_flexibility_mechanisms-41">[41]</a></sup>  The CDM is designed to encourage production of emission reductions in non-Annex I Parties, while JI encourages production of emission reductions in Annex I Parties.
</p><p>The production of emission reductions generated by the CDM and JI can be used by Annex I Parties in meeting their emission limitation commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The emission reductions produced by the CDM and JI are both measured against a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Baselines" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">baseline</a> of emissions that would have occurred in the absence of a particular emission reduction project. The emission reductions produced by the CDM are called <a href="/wiki/Certified_Emission_Reduction" title="Certified Emission Reduction">Certified Emission Reductions</a> (CERs); reductions produced by JI are called <a href="/wiki/Emission_Reduction_Unit" title="Emission Reduction Unit">Emission Reduction Units</a> (ERUs). The reductions are called "<a href="/wiki/Carbon_credit" title="Carbon credit">credits</a>" because they are emission reductions credited against a hypothetical baseline of emissions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Each Annex I country is required to submit an annual report of inventories of all anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions from sources and removals from sinks under UNFCCC and the Kyoto Protocol. These countries nominate a person (called a "designated national authority") to create and manage its <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_inventory" title="Greenhouse gas inventory">greenhouse gas inventory</a>. Virtually all of the non-Annex I countries have also established a designated national authority to manage their Kyoto obligations, specifically the "CDM process". This determines which GHG projects they wish to propose for accreditation by the CDM Executive Board.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Emissions_Trading">International Emissions Trading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: International Emissions Trading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">Emissions trading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emission_trading" title="Carbon emission trading">Carbon emission trading</a></div>
<p>A number of emissions trading schemes (ETS) have been, or are planned to be, implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19–26</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><i>Japan:</i> emissions trading in Tokyo started in 2010. This scheme is run by the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>:</i> the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Emission_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emission Trading Scheme">European Union Emission Trading Scheme</a> (EU ETS), which started in 2005. This is run by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20</span></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a></i>: domestic emissions trading in Norway started in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>21</span></sup> This was run by the Norwegian Government, which is now a participant in the EU ETS.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a></i>: the Swiss ETS, which runs from 2008 to 2012, to coincide with the Kyoto Protocol's first commitment period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>22</span></sup></li>
<li><i>United Kingdom:</i>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/UK_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="UK Emissions Trading Scheme">UK Emissions Trading Scheme</a>, which ran from 2002–06. This was a scheme run by the UK Government, which is now a participant in the EU ETS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19</span></sup></li>
<li>the UK <a href="/wiki/CRC_Energy_Efficiency_Scheme" title="CRC Energy Efficiency Scheme">CRC Energy Efficiency Scheme</a>, which started in 2010, and is run by the UK Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><i>Canada:</i> emissions trading in <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, Canada, which started in 2007. This is run by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Alberta" title="Government of Alberta">Government of Alberta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-7"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>22</span></sup></li>
<li><i>United States:</i>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Greenhouse_Gas_Initiative" title="Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative">Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative</a> (RGGI), which started in 2009. This scheme caps emissions from power generation in ten north-eastern US states (Connecticut, Delaware, Maine, Maryland, Massachusetts, New Hampshire, New Jersey, New York, Rhode Island and Vermont).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-8"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup></li>
<li>emissions trading in California, which started in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-9"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Western_Climate_Initiative" title="Western Climate Initiative">Western Climate Initiative</a> (WCI), which is planned to start in 2012. This is a collective ETS agreed between 11 US states and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_province" title="Canadian province">Canadian provinces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-10"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><i>Australia:</i> the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_Greenhouse_Gas_Abatement_Scheme" title="New South Wales Greenhouse Gas Abatement Scheme">New South Wales Greenhouse Gas Reduction Scheme</a> (NSW), which started in 2003. This scheme is run by the Australian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_New_South_Wales" title="State of New South Wales">State of New South Wales</a>, and has now joined the Alfa Climate Stabilization (ACS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-11"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19</span></sup></li>
<li><i>New Zealand</i>: the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="New Zealand Emissions Trading Scheme">New Zealand Emissions Trading Scheme</a>, which started in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hood_45-12"><a href="#cite_note-hood-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>23</span></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intergovernmental_Emissions_Trading">Intergovernmental Emissions Trading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Intergovernmental Emissions Trading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The design of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Union_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emissions Trading Scheme">European Union Emissions Trading Scheme</a> (EU ETS) implicitly allows for trade of national Kyoto obligations to occur between participating countries (Carbon Trust, 2009, p. 24).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup> Carbon Trust (2009, pp. 24–25) found that other than the trading that occurs as part of the EU ETS, no intergovernmental emissions trading had taken place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the environmental problems with IET is the large surplus of allowances that are available. Russia, Ukraine, and the new EU-12 member states (the Kyoto Parties Annex I Economies-in-Transition, abbreviated "IET": Belarus, Bulgaria, Croatia, Czech Republic, Estonia, Hungary, Latvia, Lithuania, Poland, Romania, Russia, Slovakia, Slovenia, and Ukraine)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>59</span></sup> have a surplus of allowances, while many <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries have a deficit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup>  Some of the EITs with a surplus regard it as potential compensation for the trauma of their economic restructuring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> When the Kyoto treaty was negotiated, it was recognized that emissions targets for the EITs might lead to them having an excess number of allowances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>  This excess of allowances were viewed by the EITs as "headroom" to grow their economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>  The surplus has, however, also been referred to by some as "hot air," a term which Russia (a country with an estimated surplus of 3.1 billion tonnes of carbon dioxide equivalent allowances) views as "quite offensive."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>OECD countries with a deficit could meet their Kyoto commitments by buying allowances from transition countries with a surplus. Unless other commitments were made to reduce the total surplus in allowances, such trade would not actually result in emissions being reduced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-4"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> (see also the section below on the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Green_Investment_Scheme" title="Kyoto Protocol">Green Investment Scheme</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_Investment_Scheme">Green Investment Scheme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Green Investment Scheme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A Green Investment Scheme (GIS) refers to a plan for achieving environmental benefits from trading surplus allowances (AAUs) under the Kyoto Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_Green_Investment_Scheme_(GIS)_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_Green_Investment_Scheme_(GIS)-51">[51]</a></sup> The Green Investment Scheme (GIS), a mechanism in the framework of International Emissions Trading (IET), is designed to achieve greater flexibility in reaching the targets of the Kyoto Protocol while preserving environmental integrity of IET. However, using the GIS is not required under the Kyoto Protocol, and there is no official definition of the term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_Green_Investment_Scheme_(GIS)_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_Green_Investment_Scheme_(GIS)-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the GIS a Party to the Protocol expecting that the development of its economy will not exhaust its Kyoto quota, can sell the excess of its Kyoto quota units (AAUs) to another Party. The proceeds from the AAU sales should be "greened", i.e. channeled to the development and implementation of the projects either acquiring the greenhouse gases emission reductions (hard greening) or building up the necessary framework for this process (soft greening).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-5"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_in_AAUs">Trade in AAUs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Trade in AAUs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Latvia was one of the front-runners of GISs. World Bank (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup>  reported that Latvia has stopped offering AAU sales because of low AAU prices. In 2010, Estonia was the preferred source for AAU buyers, followed by the Czech Republic and Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup>
</p><p>Japan's national policy to meet their Kyoto target includes the purchase of AAUs sold under GISs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>  In 2010, Japan and Japanese firms were the main buyers of AAUs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup>  In terms of the international carbon market, trade in AAUs are a small proportion of overall market value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>9</span></sup> In 2010, 97% of trade in the international carbon market was driven by the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Emission_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emission Trading Scheme">European Union Emission Trading Scheme</a> (EU ETS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_2011_trade_in_aaus-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>9</span></sup>  However, firms regulated under the EU ETS are unable to use AAUs in meeting their emissions caps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clean_Development_Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Clean Development Mechanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Between 2001, which was the first year <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> (CDM) projects could be registered, and 2012, the end of the first Kyoto commitment period, the CDM is expected to produce some 1.5 billion tons of carbon dioxide equivalent (CO<sub>2</sub>e) in emission reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wdr_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-wdr-55">[55]</a></sup> Most of these reductions are through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialisation" title="Renewable energy commercialisation">renewable energy commercialisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a>, and fuel switching (World Bank, 2010, p. 262). By 2012, the largest potential for production of CERs are estimated in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (52% of total CERs) and India (16%). CERs produced in Latin America and the Caribbean make up 15% of the potential total, with Brazil as the largest producer in the region (7%).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Joint_Implementation">Joint Implementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Joint Implementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The formal crediting period for <a href="/wiki/Joint_Implementation" title="Joint Implementation">Joint Implementation</a> (JI) was aligned with the first commitment period of the Kyoto Protocol, and did not start until January 2008 (Carbon Trust, 2009, p. 20).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-6"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup> In November 2008, only 22 JI projects had been officially approved and registered. The total projected emission savings from JI by 2012 are about one tenth that of the CDM. Russia accounts for about two-thirds of these savings, with the remainder divided up roughly equally between the Ukraine and the EU's New Member States. Emission savings include cuts in methane, HFC, and N<sub>2</sub>O emissions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stabilization_of_GHG_concentrations">Stabilization of GHG concentrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Stabilization of GHG concentrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As noted <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Objectives" title="Kyoto Protocol">earlier on</a>, the first-round Kyoto emissions limitation commitments are not sufficient to stabilize the atmospheric concentration of GHGs. Stabilization of atmospheric GHG concentrations will require further emissions reductions after the end of the first-round Kyoto commitment period in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_commitments_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_commitments-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_kyoto_stabilization_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_kyoto_stabilization-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_2">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
