href="#cite_note-global_warming_and_stabilization_targets-56">[56]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_global_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fossil_and_other_industrial_sources_between_2000-2100_using_MiniCAM_emissions_scenarios_from_Clarke_et_al_2007.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" data-file-height="1090" data-file-width="2045" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Projected_global_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fossil_and_other_industrial_sources_between_2000-2100_using_MiniCAM_emissions_scenarios_from_Clarke_et_al_2007.png/200px-thumbnail.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Projected_global_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fossil_and_other_industrial_sources_between_2000-2100_using_MiniCAM_emissions_scenarios_from_Clarke_et_al_2007.png/300px-thumbnail.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Projected_global_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fossil_and_other_industrial_sources_between_2000-2100_using_MiniCAM_emissions_scenarios_from_Clarke_et_al_2007.png/400px-thumbnail.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Different targets for stabilization require different levels of cuts in emissions over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emission_reductions_and_timing_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-emission_reductions_and_timing-57">[57]</a></sup> Lower stabilization targets require global emissions to be reduced more sharply in the near-term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emission_reductions_and_timing_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-emission_reductions_and_timing-57">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Analysts have developed <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_scenario" title="Climate change scenario">scenarios</a> of future changes in GHG emissions that lead to a stabilization in the atmospheric concentrations of GHGs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>  Climate models suggest that lower stabilization levels are associated with lower magnitudes of future global warming, while higher stabilization levels are associated with higher magnitudes of future global warming (see figure opposite).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-global_warming_and_stabilization_targets_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-global_warming_and_stabilization_targets-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>To achieve stabilization, global GHG emissions must peak, then decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emissions_peak_and_decline_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-emissions_peak_and_decline-59">[59]</a></sup>  The lower the desired stabilization level, the sooner this peak and decline must occur (see figure opposite).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emissions_peak_and_decline_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-emissions_peak_and_decline-59">[59]</a></sup> For a given stabilization level, larger emissions reductions in the near term allow for less stringent emissions reductions later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-near_term_emissions_reductions_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-near_term_emissions_reductions-60">[60]</a></sup> On the other hand, less stringent near term emissions reductions would, for a given stabilization level, require more stringent emissions reductions later on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-near_term_emissions_reductions_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-near_term_emissions_reductions-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first period Kyoto emissions limitations can be viewed as a first-step towards achieving atmospheric stabilization of GHGs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-21">[21]</a></sup> In this sense, the first period Kyoto commitments may affect what future atmospheric stabilization level can be achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_temperature_targets">Relation to temperature targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Relation to temperature targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2010 United Nations Climate Change Conference">16th Conference of the Parties</a> held in 2010, Parties to the UNFCCC agreed that future global warming should be limited <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference#Outcome" title="2010 United Nations Climate Change Conference">below 2°C</a> relative to the pre-industrial temperature level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>  One of the stabilization levels discussed in relation to this temperature target is to hold atmospheric concentrations of GHGs at 450 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (ppm) <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>- eq.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>  Stabilization at 450 ppm could be associated with a 26 to 78% risk of exceeding the 2 °C target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scenarios assessed by Gupta <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-450ppm_scenarios_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-450ppm_scenarios-65">[65]</a></sup> suggest that Annex I emissions would need to be 25% to 40% below 1990 levels by 2020, and 80% to 95% below 1990 levels by 2050. The only Annex I Parties to have made voluntary pledges in line with this are Japan (25% below 1990 levels by 2020) and Norway (30-40% below 1990 levels by 2020).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gupta <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-450ppm_scenarios_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-450ppm_scenarios-65">[65]</a></sup> also looked at what 450 ppm scenarios projected for non-Annex I Parties. Projections indicated that by 2020, non-Annex I emissions in several regions (<a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Planned_economy" title="Planned economy">centrally planned</a> <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>) would need to be substantially reduced below <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Baselines" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">"business-as-usual"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-450ppm_scenarios_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-450ppm_scenarios-65">[65]</a></sup> "Business-as-usual" are projected non-Annex I emissions in the absence of any new policies to control emissions. Projections indicated that by 2050, emissions in all non-Annex I regions would need to be substantially reduced below "business-as-usual".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-450ppm_scenarios_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-450ppm_scenarios-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Details_of_the_agreement">Details of the agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Details of the agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The agreement is a protocol to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) adopted at the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">Earth Summit</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a> in 1992, which did not set any legally binding limitations on emissions or enforcement mechanisms. Only Parties to the UNFCCC can become Parties to the Kyoto Protocol. The Kyoto Protocol was adopted at the third session of the Conference of Parties to the UNFCCC (COP 3) in 1997 in Kyoto, Japan.
</p><p>National emission targets specified in the Kyoto Protocol exclude international aviation and shipping. Kyoto Parties can use <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a>, land use change, and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a> (LULUCF) in meeting their targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dessai_2001_3_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dessai_2001_3-67">[67]</a></sup>  LULUCF activities are also called "sink" activities. Changes in sinks and land use can have an effect on the climate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and indeed the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change's Special Report on <a href="/wiki/Land_use,_land-use_change,_and_forestry" title="Land use, land-use change, and forestry">Land use, land-use change, and forestry</a> estimates that since 1750 a third of global warming has been caused by land use change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Particular criteria apply to the definition of forestry under the Kyoto Protocol.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Forest_management" title="Forest management">Forest management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Farm" title="Farm">cropland</a> management, <a href="/wiki/Grazing" title="Grazing">grazing</a> land management, and <a href="/wiki/Revegetation" title="Revegetation">revegetation</a> are all eligible LULUCF activities under the Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forest_management_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-forest_management-70">[70]</a></sup>  Annex I Parties use of forest management in meeting their targets is capped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forest_management_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-forest_management-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiations">Negotiations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Negotiations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Views_on_the_Kyoto_Protocol#Commentaries_on_negotiations" title="Views on the Kyoto Protocol">Views on the Kyoto Protocol § Commentaries on negotiations</a></div>
<p>Article 4.2 of the UNFCCC commits industrialized countries to "[take] the lead" in reducing emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_original_unfccc_target_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_original_unfccc_target-71">[71]</a></sup> The initial aim was for industrialized countries to stabilize their emissions at 1990 levels by the year 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_original_unfccc_target_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_original_unfccc_target-71">[71]</a></sup> The failure of key industrialized countries to move in this direction was a principal reason why Kyoto moved to binding commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_original_unfccc_target_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_original_unfccc_target-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the first UNFCCC Conference of the Parties in Berlin, the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_77" title="Group of 77">G77</a> was able to push for a mandate (the "Berlin mandate") where it was recognized that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_berlin_mandate_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_berlin_mandate-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>developed nations had contributed most to the then-current concentrations of GHGs in the atmosphere (see <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Cumulative_and_historical_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas#Cumulative and historical emissions</a>).</li>
<li>developing country <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Regional_and_national_attribution_of_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">emissions per-capita</a> (i.e., average emissions per head of population)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> were still relatively low.</li>
<li>and that the share of global emissions from developing countries would grow to meet their development needs.</li></ul>
<p>During negotiations, the G-77 represented 133 developing countries. China was not a member of the group but an associate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> It has since become a member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Berlin mandate was recognized in the Kyoto Protocol in that developing countries were not subject to emission reduction commitments in the first Kyoto commitment period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_berlin_mandate_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_berlin_mandate-72">[72]</a></sup> However, the large potential for growth in developing country emissions made negotiations on this issue tense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_developing_country_emissions_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_developing_country_emissions-76">[76]</a></sup> In the final agreement, the Clean Development Mechanism was designed to limit emissions in developing countries, but in such a way that developing countries do not bear the costs for limiting emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_developing_country_emissions_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_developing_country_emissions-76">[76]</a></sup> The general assumption was that developing countries would face quantitative commitments in later commitment periods, and at the same time, developed countries would meet their first round commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_developing_country_emissions_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_developing_country_emissions-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions_cuts">Emissions cuts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Emissions cuts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Views_on_the_Kyoto_Protocol#Commentaries_on_negotiations" title="Views on the Kyoto Protocol">Views on the Kyoto Protocol#Commentaries on negotiations</a> contains a list of the emissions cuts that were proposed by UNFCCC Parties during negotiations. The G77 and China were in favour of strong uniform emission cuts across the developed world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_negotiations_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_negotiations-77">[77]</a></sup> The US originally proposed for the second round of negotiations on Kyoto commitments to follow the negotiations of the first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_second_round_negotiations_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_second_round_negotiations-78">[78]</a></sup> In the end, negotiations on the second period were set to open no later than 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_second_round_negotiations_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_second_round_negotiations-78">[78]</a></sup> Countries over-achieving in their first period commitments can "bank" their unused allowances for use in the subsequent period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_second_round_negotiations_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_second_round_negotiations-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The EU initially argued for only three GHGs to be included – <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> – with other gases such as HFCs regulated separately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_negotiations_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_negotiations-77">[77]</a></sup> The EU also wanted to have a "bubble" commitment, whereby it could make a collective commitment that allowed some EU members to increase their emissions, while others cut theirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_negotiations_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_negotiations-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most vulnerable nations – the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Small_Island_States" title="Alliance of Small Island States">Alliance of Small Island States</a> (AOSIS) – pushed for deep uniform cuts by developed nations, with the goal of having emissions reduced to the greatest possible extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_negotiations_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_negotiations-77">[77]</a></sup> Countries that had supported differentiation of targets had different ideas as to how it should be calculated, and many different indicators were proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_differentiation_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_differentiation-79">[79]</a></sup> Two examples include differentiation of targets based on <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP), and differentiation based on <a href="/wiki/Energy_intensity" title="Energy intensity">energy intensity</a> (energy use per unit of economic output).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_differentiation_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_differentiation-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final targets negotiated in the Protocol are the result of last minute political compromises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_negotiations_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-liverman_negotiations-77">[77]</a></sup> The targets closely match those decided by Argentinian Raul Estrada, the <a href="/wiki/Diplomat" title="Diplomat">diplomat</a> who chaired the negotiations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>  The numbers given to each Party by Chairman Estrada were based on targets already pledged by Parties, information received on latest negotiating positions, and the goal of achieving the strongest possible environmental outcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The final targets are weaker than those proposed by some Parties, e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Small_Island_States" title="Alliance of Small Island States">Alliance of Small Island States</a> and the G-77 and China, but stronger than the targets proposed by others, e.g., Canada and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_commitments">Financial commitments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Financial commitments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Protocol also reaffirms the principle that developed countries have to pay billions of dollars, and supply technology to other countries for climate-related studies and projects. The principle was originally agreed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFCCC" title="UNFCCC">UNFCCC</a>. One such project is <a href="/wiki/The_Adaptation_Fund" title="The Adaptation Fund">The Adaptation Fund</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>", that has been established by the Parties to the Kyoto Protocol of the UN Framework Convention on Climate Change to finance concrete adaptation projects and programmes in developing countries that are Parties to the Kyoto Protocol.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Implementational_provisions">Implementational provisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Implementational provisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The protocol left several issues open to be decided later by the sixth Conference of Parties <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_conference#2000:_COP_6,_The_Hague,_Netherlands" title="United Nations Climate Change conference">COP6</a> of the UNFCCC, which attempted to resolve these issues at its meeting in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">the Hague</a> in late 2000, but it was unable to reach an agreement due to disputes between the European Union (who favoured a tougher implementation) and the United States, Canada, Japan and Australia (who wanted the agreement to be less demanding and more flexible).
</p><p>In 2001, a continuation of the previous meeting (COP6bis) was held in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> where the required decisions were adopted. After some concessions, the supporters of the protocol (led by the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>) managed to get the agreement of Japan and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> by allowing more use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2_sink" title="CO2 sink">carbon dioxide sinks</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_conference#2001:_COP_7,_Marrakech,_Morocco" title="United Nations Climate Change conference">COP7</a> was held from 29 October 2001 through 9 November 2001 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marrakech" title="Marrakech">Marrakech</a> to establish the final details of the protocol.
</p><p>The first Meeting of the Parties to the Kyoto Protocol (MOP1) was held in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> from 28 November to 9 December 2005, along with the 11th conference of the Parties to the UNFCCC (COP11). See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="United Nations Climate Change Conference">United Nations Climate Change Conference</a>.
</p><p>During COP13 in Bali, 36 developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contact_Group" title="Contact Group">Contact Group</a> countries (plus the EU as a party in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>) agreed to a 10% emissions increase for <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>; but, since the EU's member states each have individual obligations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> much larger increases (up to 27%) are allowed for some of the less developed EU countries (see below <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Increase_in_greenhouse_gas_emission_since_1990" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol#Increase in greenhouse gas emission since 1990</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Reduction limitations expired in 2013.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_compliance">Mechanism of compliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Mechanism of compliance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The protocol defines a mechanism of "compliance" as a "monitoring compliance with the commitments and penalties for non-compliance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> According to Grubb (2003),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_compliance_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_compliance-87">[87]</a></sup> the explicit consequences of non-compliance of the treaty are weak compared to domestic law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_compliance_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_compliance-87">[87]</a></sup> Yet, the compliance section of the treaty was highly contested in the Marrakesh Accords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_compliance_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_compliance-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforcement">Enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If the enforcement branch determines that an Annex I country is not in compliance with its emissions limitation, then that country is required to make up the difference during the second commitment period plus an additional 30%.  In addition, that country will be suspended from making transfers under an emissions trading program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratification_process">Ratification process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ratification process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Protocol was adopted by <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">COP 3</a> of UNFCCC on 11 December 1997 in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. It was opened on 16 March 1998 for signature during one year by parties to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFCCC" title="UNFCCC">UNFCCC</a>, when it was signed Antigua and Barbuda, Argentina, the Maldives, Samoa, St. Lucia and Switzerland. At the end of the signature period, 82 countries and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Community" title="European Community">European Community</a> had signed. <a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">Ratification</a> (which is required to become a party to the Protocol) started on 17 September with ratification of Fiji. Countries that did not sign acceded to the convention, which has the same legal effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parties_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-parties-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Article 25 of the Protocol specifies that the Protocol enters into force "on the ninetieth day after the date on which not less than 55 Parties to the Convention, incorporating Parties included in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annex_I" title="Annex I">Annex I</a> which accounted in total for at least 55% of the total carbon dioxide emissions for 1990 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annex_I" title="Annex I">Annex I</a> countries, have deposited their instruments of ratification, acceptance, approval or accession."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The EU and its Member States ratified the Protocol in May 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Of the two conditions, the "55 parties" clause was reached on 23 May 2002 when <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> ratified the Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parties_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-parties-1">[1]</a></sup> The ratification by <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> on 18 November 2004 satisfied the "55%" clause and brought the treaty into force, effective 16 February 2005, after the required lapse of 90 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of May 2013, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Kyoto_Protocol_signatories" title="List of Kyoto Protocol signatories">191 countries</a> and one regional economic organization (the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">EC</a>) have ratified the agreement, representing over 61.6% of the 1990 emissions from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annex_I" title="Annex I">Annex I</a> countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kyoto-PDF-unfccc_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kyoto-PDF-unfccc-92">[92]</a></sup> One of the 191 ratifying states—Canada—has renounced the protocol.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-ratification_by_the_US">Non-ratification by the US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Non-ratification by the US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The US signed the Protocol on 12 November 1998,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> during the <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Clinton</a> presidency. To become binding in the US, however, the treaty had to be ratified by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>, which had already passed the 1997 non-binding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byrd-Hagel_Resolution" title="Byrd-Hagel Resolution">Byrd-Hagel Resolution</a>, expressing disapproval of any international agreement that did not require developing countries to make emission reductions and "would seriously harm the economy of the United States".  The resolution passed 95–0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Therefore, even though the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinton_administration" title="Clinton administration">Clinton administration</a> signed the treaty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> it was never submitted to the Senate for ratification.
</p><p>
When <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> was elected US president in 2000, he was asked by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Senator" title="US Senator">US Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Hagel" title="Chuck Hagel">Chuck Hagel</a> what his administration's position was on climate change. Bush replied that he took climate change "very seriously",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> but that he opposed the Kyoto treaty because "it exempts 80% of the world, including major population centers such as China and India, from compliance, and would cause serious harm to the US economy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dessai_2001_5_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dessai_2001_5-97">[97]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tyndall_Centre" title="Tyndall Centre">Tyndall Centre</a> for Climate Change Research reported in 2001:</p><blockquote><p>This policy reversal received a massive wave of criticism that was quickly picked up by the international media. Environmental groups blasted the White House, while Europeans and Japanese alike expressed deep concern and regret. [...] Almost all world leaders (e.g. China, Japan, South Africa, Pacific Islands, etc.) expressed their disappointment at Bush's decision.</p></blockquote><p>In response to this criticism, Bush stated: "I was responding to reality, and reality is the nation has got a real problem when it comes to energy." The Tyndall Centre called this "an overstatement used to cover up the big benefactors of this policy reversal, i.e., the US oil and coal industry, which has a powerful lobby with the administration and conservative <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> congressmen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2016, the US is the only signatory that has not ratified the Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The US accounted for 36% of emissions in 1990. As such, for the treaty to go into legal effect without US ratification, it would require a coalition including the EU, Russia, Japan, and small parties. A deal, without the US Administration, was reached in the Bonn climate talks (COP-6.5), held in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawal_of_Canada">Withdrawal of Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Withdrawal of Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action#Withdrawal_of_Canada" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">Kyoto Protocol and government action § Withdrawal of Canada</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Canada_and_the_Kyoto_Protocol" title="Canada and the Kyoto Protocol">Canada and the Kyoto Protocol</a></div>
<p>In 2011, Canada, Japan and Russia stated that they would not take on further Kyoto targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canadaguardian_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-canadaguardian-101">[101]</a></sup> The Canadian government announced its withdrawal—possible at any time three years after ratification—from the Kyoto Protocol on 12 December 2011, effective 15 December 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Canada was committed to cutting its greenhouse emissions to 6% below 1990 levels by 2012, but in 2009 emissions were 17% higher than in 1990. The <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harper" title="Stephen Harper">Harper</a> government prioritized oil sands development in Alberta, and deprioritized improving the environment. Environment minister <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kent" title="Peter Kent">Peter Kent</a> cited Canada's liability to "enormous financial penalties" under the treaty unless it withdrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canadaguardian_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-canadaguardian-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>  He also suggested that the recently signed <a href="/wiki/2011_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2011 United Nations Climate Change Conference">Durban agreement</a> may provide an alternative way forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_canada_withdrawal_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_canada_withdrawal-104">[104]</a></sup> The Harper government claimed it would find a "Made in Canada" solution, but never found any such solution. Canada's decision received a generally negative response from representatives of other ratifying countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_canada_withdrawal_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_canada_withdrawal-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_states_and_territories_where_the_treaty_is_not_applicable">Other states and territories where the treaty is not applicable</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other states and territories where the treaty is not applicable">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Andorra, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, the United States and, following their withdrawal on 15 December 2012, Canada are the only UNFCCC Parties that are not party to the Protocol. Furthermore, the Protocol is not applied to UNFCCC observer the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>. Although the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="Kingdom of the Netherlands">Kingdom of the Netherlands</a> approved the protocol for the whole Kingdom, it did not deposit an instrument of ratification for Aruba, Curaçao, Sint Maarten or the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Netherlands" title="Caribbean Netherlands">Caribbean Netherlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_action_and_emissions">Government action and emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Government action and emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">Kyoto Protocol and government action</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions_per_capita" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita">List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_ratio_of_GDP_to_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by ratio of GDP to carbon dioxide emissions">List of countries by ratio of GDP to carbon dioxide emissions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annex_I_countries">Annex I countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Annex I countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2emissions1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="608" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/CO2emissions1.jpg/220px-CO2emissions1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/CO2emissions1.jpg/330px-CO2emissions1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/CO2emissions1.jpg/440px-CO2emissions1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2emissions1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anthropogenic emissions of CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalents per year by the 10 largest emitters (the European Union is lumped as a single area, because of their integrated carbon trading scheme). Data sorted based on 2010 contributions. <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF6600; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> China (party, no binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> United States (non-party)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFF00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> European Union (party, binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #616228; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> India (party, no binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Russia (party, binding targets 2008-2012)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #CCFFCC; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Indonesia (party, no binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #948A54; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Brazil (party, no binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #1FE9FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Japan (party, no binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #AD9618; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Congo (DR) (party, no binding targets)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #9537DA; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Canada (former party, binding targets 2008-2012)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #A6A6A6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Other countries</div></div></div></div>
<p>Total aggregate GHG emissions excluding emissions/removals from land use, land use change and forestry (LULUCF, i.e., carbon storage in forests and soils) for all Annex I Parties (see list below) including the United States taken together decreased from 19.0 to 17.8 thousand <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(mass)" title="Orders of magnitude (mass)">teragrams</a> (Tg, which is equal to 10<sup>9</sup> kg) <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> equivalent, a decline of 6.0% during the 1990-2008 period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup>  Several factors have contributed to this decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup>  The first is due to the economic restructuring in the Annex I Economies in Transition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup>  (the EITs – see <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Intergovernmental_Emissions_Trading" title="Kyoto Protocol">Intergovernmental Emissions Trading</a> for the list of EITs). Over the period 1990-1999, emissions fell by 40% in the EITs following the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Planned_economy" title="Planned economy">central planning</a> in the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">east European</a> countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup>  This led to a massive contraction of their heavy industry-based economies, with associated reductions in their fossil fuel consumption and emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market_46-7"><a href="#cite_note-2009_carbon_trust_carbon_market-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup>
</p><p>Emissions growth in Annex I Parties have also been limited due to policies and measures (PaMs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> In particular, PaMs were strengthened after 2000, helping to enhance energy efficiency and develop renewable energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> Energy use also decreased during the economic crisis in 2007-2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-5"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections">Projections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Projections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>UNFCCC (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-6"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> made projections of changes in emissions of the Annex I Parties and the effectiveness of their PaMs. It was noted that their projections should be interpreted with caution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-7"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup>  For the 39 Annex I Parties, UNFCCC (2011) projected that existing PaMs would lead to annual emissions in 2010 of 17.5 thousand Tg <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> eq, excluding LULUCF, which is a decrease of 6.7% from the 1990 level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-8"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> Annual emissions in 2020 excluding LULUCF were projected to reach 18.9 thousand Tg <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> eq, which is an increase of 0.6% on the 1990 level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-9"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup>
</p><p>UNFCCC (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-10"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> made an estimate of the total effect of implemented and adopted PaMs. Projected savings were estimated relative to a <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Baselines" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">reference (baseline) scenario</a> where PaMs are not implemented.  PaMs were projected to deliver emissions savings relative to baseline of about 1.5 thousand Tg <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> eq by 2010, and 2.8 thousand Tg <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> eq by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-11"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup>  In percentage terms, and using annual emissions in the year 1990 as a reference point, PaMs were projected to deliver at least a 5.0% reduction relative to baseline by 2010, and a 10.0% reduction relative to baseline in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-12"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> Scenarios reviewed by UNFCCC (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_106-13"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14</span></sup> still suggested that total Annex I annual emissions would increase out to 2020 (see the preceding paragraph).
</p>

<p><i>Data given in the table above may not be fully reflective of a country's progress towards meeting its first-round Kyoto target. The summary below contains more up-to-date information on how close countries are to meeting their first-round targets.</i>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png"><img alt="Refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="1077" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png/220px-Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png/330px-Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png/440px-Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_of_Annex_I_Kyoto_Protocol_Parties_1990-2009.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from fuel combustion of Annex I Kyoto Protocol (KP) Parties, 1990-2009. Total Annex I KP emissions are shown, along with emissions of Annex II KP and Annex I EITs.</div></div></div>
<p>Collectively the group of industrialized countries committed to a Kyoto target, i.e., the Annex I countries excluding the US, have a target of reducing their GHG emissions by 4.2% on average for the period 2008-2012 relative to the base year, which in most cases is 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup>  According to Olivier <i>et al.</i> (2011),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup> the Kyoto Parties will comfortably exceed their collective target, with a projected average reduction of 16% for 2008-2012. This projection excludes both LULUCF and credits generated by the Clean Development Mechanism (CDM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>24</span></sup>
</p><p>As noted in the preceding section, between 1990–1999, there was a large reduction in the emissions of the EITs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> The reduction in the EITs is largely responsible for the total (aggregate) reduction (excluding LULUCF) in emissions of the Annex I countries, excluding the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-5"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> Emissions of the Annex II countries (Annex I minus the EIT countries) have experienced a limited increase in emissions from 1990–2006, followed by stabilization and a more marked decrease from 2007 onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-6"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> The emissions reductions in the early nineties by the 12 EIT countries who have since joined the EU, assist the present EU-27 in meeting its collective Kyoto target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-7"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup>
</p><p>Almost all European countries are on track to achieve their first-round Kyoto targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eea_1st_round_kyoto_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-eea_1st_round_kyoto-118">[118]</a></sup> Spain plans to meet its target by purchasing a large quantity of Kyoto units through the flexibility mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eea_1st_round_kyoto_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-eea_1st_round_kyoto-118">[118]</a></sup> Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-8"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> Canada<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-9"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> (Canada withdrew from the Kyoto treaty in 2012),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaughan_2011_canada_withdrawal_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan_2011_canada_withdrawal-119">[119]</a></sup> and Italy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eea_1st_round_kyoto_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-eea_1st_round_kyoto-118">[118]</a></sup> are not on course to meet their first-round Kyoto targets. In order to meet their targets, these countries would need to purchase emissions credits from other Kyoto countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-10"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup> As noted in the section on <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Intergovernmental_Emissions_Trading" title="Kyoto Protocol">Intergovernmental Emissions Trading</a>, purchasing surplus credits from the EIT countries would not actually result in total emissions being reduced. An alternative would be the purchase of CDM credits or the use of the voluntary Green Investment Scheme.
</p><p>In December 2011, Canada's environment minister, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kent" title="Peter Kent">Peter Kent</a>, formally announced that <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Canada" title="Climate change in Canada">Canada</a> would withdraw from the Kyoto accord a day after the end of the <a href="/wiki/2011_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2011 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2011 United Nations Climate Change Conference</a> (see the section on the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Withdrawal_of_Canada" title="Kyoto Protocol">withdrawal of Canada</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaughan_2011_canada_withdrawal_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan_2011_canada_withdrawal-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Annex_I_Parties_without_Kyoto_targets">Annex I Parties without Kyoto targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Annex I Parties without Kyoto targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Belarus, Malta, and Turkey are Annex I Parties but do not have first-round Kyoto targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The US has a Kyoto target of a 6% reduction relative to the 1990 level, but has not ratified the treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-11"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup>  Emissions in the US have increased 11% since 1990, and according to Olivier <i>et al.</i> (2011),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-12"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>25</span></sup>  it will be unable to meet its original Kyoto target.
</p><p>If the US had ratified the Kyoto Protocol, the average percentage reduction in total GHG emissions for the Annex I group would have been a 5.2% reduction relative to the base year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-13"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup> Including the US in their calculation, Olivier <i>et al.</i> (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-14"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup>  projected that the Annex I countries would collectively achieve a 7% reduction relative to the base year, which is lower than the original target of a 5.2% reduction. This projection excludes expected purchases of emissions credits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions_107-15"><a href="#cite_note-Long_term_trend_in_global_CO2_emissions-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Annex_I">Non-Annex I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Non-Annex I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_per_capita_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" data-file-height="738"
