2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Annual per capita carbon dioxide emissions (i.e., average emissions per person) from fuel combustion between 1990-2009 for the Kyoto Annex I and non-Annex I Parties.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="1056" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png/200px-Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png/300px-Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png/400px-Annual_carbon_dioxide_emissions_from_fuel_combustion_between_1990-2009_for_the_Kyoto_Annex_I_and_non-Annex_I_Parties.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Annual carbon dioxide emissions from fuel combustion between 1990-2009 for the Kyoto Annex I and non-Annex I Parties.</div></div></div></div>
<p>UNFCCC (2005) compiled and synthesized information reported to it by non-Annex I Parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary-33">[33]</a></sup> Most non-Annex I Parties belonged in the low-income group, with very few classified as middle-income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup> Most Parties included information on policies relating to <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>. Sustainable development priorities mentioned by non-Annex I Parties included poverty alleviation and access to basic education and health care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup> Many non-Annex I Parties are making efforts to amend and update their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_legislation" title="Environmental legislation">environmental legislation</a> to include global concerns such as climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup>
</p><p>A few Parties, e.g., South Africa and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, stated their concern over how efforts to reduce emissions by Annex I Parties could adversely affect their economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-2005_unfccc_non-annex_i_summary-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup> The economies of these countries are highly dependent on income generated from the production, processing, and export of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.
</p><p><b>Emissions</b>
</p><p>GHG emissions, excluding land use change and forestry (LUCF), reported by 122 non-Annex I Parties for the year 1994 or the closest year reported, totalled 11.7 billion tonnes (billion = 1,000,000,000) of CO<sub>2</sub>-eq. CO<sub>2</sub> was the largest proportion of emissions (63%), followed by <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (26%) and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O) (11%).
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_sector" title="Energy sector">energy sector</a> was the largest source of emissions for 70 Parties, whereas for 45 Parties the agriculture sector was the largest. Per capita emissions (in tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub>-eq, excluding LUCF) averaged 2.8 tonnes for the 122 non-Annex I Parties.
</p>
<ul><li>The Africa region's <a href="/wiki/Aggregate_data" title="Aggregate data">aggregate</a> emissions were 1.6 billion tonnes, with per capita emissions of 2.4 tonnes.</li>
<li>The Asia and Pacific region's aggregate emissions were 7.9 billion tonnes, with per capita emissions of 2.6 tonnes.</li>
<li>The Latin America and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> region's aggregate emissions were 2 billion tonnes, with per capita emissions of 4.6 tonnes.</li>
<li>The "other" region includes <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, Armenia, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, Georgia, Malta, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Moldova" title="Republic of Moldova">Moldova</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Macedonia</a>. Their aggregate emissions were 0.1 billion tonnes, with per capita emissions of 5.1 tonnes.</li></ul>
<p>Parties reported a high level of uncertainty in LUCF emissions, but in aggregate, there appeared to only be a small difference of 1.7% with and without LUCF. With LUCF, emissions were 11.9 billion tonnes, without LUCF, total aggregate emissions were 11.7 billion tonnes.
</p><p><i>Trends</i>
</p><p>In several large developing countries and fast growing economies (China, India, Thailand, Indonesia, Egypt, and Iran) GHG emissions have increased rapidly (PBL, 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>  For example, emissions in China have risen strongly over the 1990–2005 period, often by more than 10% year. Emissions per-capita in non-Annex I countries are still, for the most part, much lower than in industrialized countries. Non-Annex I countries do not have quantitative emission reduction commitments, but they are committed to mitigation actions. China, for example, has had a national policy programme to reduce emissions growth, which included the closure of old, less efficient coal-fired power plants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_estimates">Cost estimates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Cost estimates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Barker <i>et al.</i> (2007, p. 79) assessed the literature on cost estimates for the Kyoto Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Due to non-US participation in the Kyoto treaty, costs estimates were found to be much lower than those estimated in the previous <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Third_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Third Assessment Report">IPCC Third Assessment Report</a>. Without US participation, and with full use of the Kyoto flexible mechanisms, costs were estimated at less than 0.05% of Annex B GDP. This compared to earlier estimates of 0.1–1.1%. Without use of the flexible mechanisms, costs without US participation were estimated at less than 0.1%. This compared to earlier estimates of 0.2–2%. These cost estimates were viewed as being based on much evidence and high agreement in the literature.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_the_Protocol">Views on the Protocol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Views on the Protocol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Views_on_the_Kyoto_Protocol" title="Views on the Kyoto Protocol">Views on the Kyoto Protocol</a></div>
<p>Gupta <i>et al.</i> (2007) assessed the literature on climate change policy. They found that no authoritative assessments of the UNFCCC or its Protocol asserted that these agreements had, or will, succeed in solving the climate problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-21">[21]</a></sup> In these assessments, it was assumed that the UNFCCC or its Protocol would not be changed. The Framework Convention and its Protocol include provisions for future policy actions to be taken.
</p><p>Gupta <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_kyoto_assessment_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_kyoto_assessment-123">[123]</a></sup>  described the Kyoto first-round commitments as "modest," stating that they acted as a constraint on the treaty's effectiveness. It was suggested that subsequent Kyoto commitments could be made more effective with measures aimed at achieving deeper cuts in emissions, as well as having policies applied to a larger share of global emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_kyoto_assessment_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-gupta_kyoto_assessment-123">[123]</a></sup> In 2008, countries with a Kyoto cap made up less than one-third of annual global carbon dioxide emissions from fuel <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>World Bank (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_kyoto_comments_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_kyoto_comments-125">[125]</a></sup> commented on how the Kyoto Protocol had only had a slight effect on curbing global emissions growth. The treaty was negotiated in 1997, but in 2006, energy-related carbon dioxide emissions had grown by 24%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> World Bank (2010) also stated that the treaty had provided only limited financial support to developing countries to assist them in reducing their emissions and adapting to climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_kyoto_comments_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_kyoto_comments-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Kyoto_Protocol" title="Criticism of the Kyoto Protocol">criticism of the Protocol</a> has been based on the idea of climate justice (Liverman, 2008, p. 14).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-liverman-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>
This has particularly centered on the balance between the low emissions and high vulnerability of the developing world to climate change, compared to high emissions in the developed world. Another criticism of the Kyoto Protocol and other international conventions, is the right of indigenous peoples right to participate. Quoted here from The Declaration of the <a href="/wiki/International_Indigenous_Peoples_Forum_on_Climate_Change" title="International Indigenous Peoples Forum on Climate Change">First International Forum of Indigenous Peoples on Climate Change</a>, it says "Despite the recognition of our role in preventing global warming, when it comes time to sign international conventions like the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change, once again, our right to participate in national and international discussions that directly affect or Peoples and territories is denied."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-127">[127]</a></sup> Additionally, later in the declaration, it reads </p><blockquote><p>"We denounce the fact that neither the [United Nations] nor the Kyoto Protocol recognizes the existence or the contributions of Indigenous Peoples. Furthermore, the debates under these instruments have not considered the suggestions and proposals of the Indigenous Peoples nor have the appropriate mechanisms to guarantee our participation in all the debates that directly concern the Indigenous Peoples has been established."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-127">[127]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Some environmentalists have supported the Kyoto Protocol because it is "the only game in town," and possibly because they expect that future emission reduction commitments may demand more stringent emission reductions (Aldy <i>et al.</i>., 2003, p. 9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aldy_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-aldy-128">[128]</a></sup> In 2001, seventeen national science academies stated that ratification of the Protocol represented a "small but essential first step towards stabilising atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Some environmentalists and scientists have criticized the existing commitments for being too weak (Grubb, 2000, p. 5).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States (under former President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>) and Australia (initially, under former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/John_Howard" title="John Howard">John Howard</a>) did not ratify the Kyoto treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern_us_and_australia_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern_us_and_australia-131">[131]</a></sup> According to Stern (2006),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern_us_and_australia_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-stern_us_and_australia-131">[131]</a></sup> their decision was based on the lack of quantitative emission commitments for emerging economies (see also the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#2000_onwards" title="Kyoto Protocol">2000 onwards</a> section). Australia, under former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Rudd" title="Kevin Rudd">Kevin Rudd</a>, has since ratified the treaty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-australia_signing_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-australia_signing-133">[133]</a></sup> which took effect in March 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_the_flexibility_mechanisms">Views on the flexibility mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Views on the flexibility mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Flexible_Mechanisms#Views_on_the_flexibility_mechanisms" title="Flexible Mechanisms">Flexible Mechanisms § Views on the flexibility mechanisms</a></div>
<p>Another area which has been commented on is the role of the Kyoto <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flexibility_mechanisms" title="Flexibility mechanisms">flexibility mechanisms</a> – <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emissions trading</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joint_Implementation" title="Joint Implementation">Joint Implementation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> (CDM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toth_flexibility_mechanisms_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-toth_flexibility_mechanisms-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The flexibility mechanisms have attracted both positive and negative comments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>As mentioned earlier, a number of Annex I Parties have implemented emissions trading schemes (ETSs) as part of efforts to meet their Kyoto commitments. General commentaries on emissions trading are contained in <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">emissions trading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emission_trading" title="Carbon emission trading">carbon emission trading</a>. Individual articles on the ETSs contain commentaries on these schemes (see <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#International_Emissions_Trading" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol#International Emissions Trading</a> for a list of ETSs).
</p><p>One of the arguments made in favour of the flexibility mechanisms is that they can reduce the costs incurred by Annex I Parties in meeting their Kyoto commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toth_flexibility_mechanisms_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-toth_flexibility_mechanisms-135">[135]</a></sup> Criticisms of flexibility have, for example, included the ineffectiveness of emissions trading in promoting investment in non-fossil energy sources,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and adverse impacts of CDM projects on local communities in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the Kyoto Protocol seeks to reduce environmental pollutants while at the same time altering the freedoms of some citizens.
</p><p>As discussed by <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a>, one can achieve both economic and political freedom through capitalism; nonetheless, it is never guaranteed that one is going to have equality of wealth of those on top of the “food chain” of this capitalistic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> All these alterations come to what the leaders of the citizens choose to impose in means of improving ones lifestyle. In the case of the Kyoto Protocol, it seeks to impose regulations that will reduce production of pollutants towards the   environment. Furthermore, seeking to compromise the freedoms of both private and public citizens. In one side it imposes bigger regulations towards companies and reducing their profits as they need to fulfill such regulations with, which are oftentimes more expensive, alternatives for production. On the other hand, it seeks to reduce the emissions that potentially cause the rapid environmental change called <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.
</p><p>The conditions of the Kyoto Protocol consist of mandatory targets on greenhouse gas emissions for the world's leading economies. As provided by the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change, "These targets range from -8 per cent to +10 per cent of the countries' individual 1990 emissions levels with a view to reducing their overall emissions of such gases by at least 5 per cent below existing 1990 levels in the commitment period 2008 to 2012." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>These goals are challenged, however, by climate change deniers, who condemn strong scientific evidence of the human impact on climate change. One prominent scholar opines that these climate change deniers "arguably" breach Rousseau's notion of the social contract, which is an implicit agreement among the members of a society to coordinate efforts in the name of overall social benefit. The climate change denial movement hinders efforts at coming to agreements as a collective global society on climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conference_of_the_Parties">Conference of the Parties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Conference of the Parties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_conference" title="United Nations Climate Change conference">United Nations Climate Change conference</a></div>
<p>The official meeting of all states party to the Kyoto Protocol is the <i>Conference of the Parties</i>. It is held every year as part of the United Nations Climate Change conference, which also serves as the formal meeting of UNFCCC. The first Meetings of Parties of the Kyoto Protocol (CMP) was held in 2005 in conjunction with the eleventh Conferences of parties to UNFCCC (COP). Also parties to the Convention that are not parties to the Protocol can participate in Protocol-related meetings as observers. The first conference was held in 1995 in Berlin, while the 2013 conference was held in <a href="/wiki/2013_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2013 United Nations Climate Change Conference">Warsaw</a>. Later COPs were held in Lima, Peru in 2014 and in Paris, France in 2015. COP 21 aims to hold the increase in the global average rise in temperature below 2 degree. COP 22 Marakash, Morocco and COP 23 in Bonn, Germany.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Amendment_and_possible_successors">Amendment and possible successors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Amendment and possible successors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93Kyoto_Protocol_negotiations_on_greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Post–Kyoto Protocol negotiations on greenhouse gas emissions">Post–Kyoto Protocol negotiations on greenhouse gas emissions</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b> with: <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(December 2015)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the non-binding "<a href="/wiki/G8%2B5" title="G8+5">Washington Declaration</a>" agreed on 16 February 2007, heads of governments from Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, Russia, the United Kingdom, the United States, Brazil, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> agreed in principle on the outline of a successor to the Kyoto Protocol. They envisaged a global cap-and-trade system that would apply to both industrialized nations and <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, and initially hoped that it would be in place by 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations Climate Change Conference in Copenhagen in December 2009 was one of the annual series of UN meetings that followed the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio. In 1997 the talks led to the Kyoto Protocol, and the conference in Copenhagen was considered to be the opportunity to agree a successor to Kyoto that would bring about meaningful carbon cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2010 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2010 Cancún agreements</a> include voluntary pledges made by 76 developed and developing countries to control their emissions of greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king_2011_cancun_agreement_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-king_2011_cancun_agreement-149">[149]</a></sup> In 2010, these 76 countries were collectively responsible for 85% of annual global emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king_2011_cancun_agreement_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-king_2011_cancun_agreement-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unep_2012_emissions_gap_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-unep_2012_emissions_gap-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>By May 2012, the US, Japan, Russia, and Canada had indicated they would not sign up to a second Kyoto commitment period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In November 2012, Australia confirmed it would participate in a second commitment period under the Kyoto Protocol and New Zealand confirmed that it would not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>New Zealand's climate minister Tim Groser said the 15-year-old Kyoto Protocol was outdated, and that New Zealand was "ahead of the curve" in looking for a replacement that would include developing nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Non-profit environmental organisations such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a> criticised New Zealand's decision to pull out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 8 December 2012, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/2012_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2012 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2012 United Nations Climate Change Conference</a>, an agreement was reached to extend the Protocol to 2020 and to set a date of 2015 for the development of a successor document, to be implemented from 2020 (see lede for more information).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The outcome of the Doha talks has received a mixed response, with small island states critical of the overall package.The Kyoto second commitment period applies to about 11% of annual global emissions of greenhouse gases. Other results of the conference include a timetable for a global agreement to be adopted by 2015 which includes all countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc_2012_doha_press_release_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc_2012_doha_press_release-156">[156]</a></sup> At the Doha meeting of the parties to the UNFCCC on 8 December 2012, the European Union chief climate negotiator, Artur Runge-Metzger, pledged to extend the treaty, binding on the 27 European Member States, up to the year 2020 pending an internal ratification procedure.
</p><p>Ban Ki Moon, Secretary General of the United Nations, called  on world leaders to come to an agreement on halting global warming during the 69th Session of the UN General Assembly<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> on 23 September 2014 in New York. UN member states have been negotiating a future climate deal over the last five years. A preliminary calendar was adopted to confirm "national contributions" to the reduction of CO2 emissions by 2015 before the UN climate summit which was held in Paris at the  <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<cite class="citation" id="CITEREFUnited_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change_(UNFCCC)2011a"><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) (2011a), <a class="external text" href="http://unfccc.int/essential_background/convention/status_of_ratification/items/2631.php" rel="nofollow"><i>Status of Ratification of the Convention</i></a>, UNFCCC Secretariat: <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>: UNFCCC</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Status+of+Ratification+of+the+Convention&amp;rft.place=UNFCCC+Secretariat%3A+Bonn%2C+Germany&amp;rft.pub=UNFCCC&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.au=United+Nations+Framework+Convention+on+Climate+Change+%28UNFCCC%29&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Funfccc.int%2Fessential_background%2Fconvention%2Fstatus_of_ratification%2Fitems%2F2631.php&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKyoto+Protocol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>. Most countries in the world are Parties to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC), which has adopted the 2 °C target. There are currently (as of November 25, 2011) 195 Parties (194 states and 1 regional economic integration organization (the European Union)) to the UNFCCC.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-2001d_ipcc_article_2_of_UNFCCC_18-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
<cite class="citation book">IPCC (2001d). "Question 1".  In Watson, R.T.; the Core Writing Team. <a class="external text" href="http://www.grida.no/climate/ipcc_tar/vol4/english/017.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>Climate Change 2001: Synthesis Report. A Contribution of Working Groups I, II, and III to the Third Assessment Report of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC)</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
The benchmark 1990 emission levels accepted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">Conference of the parties</a> of UNFCCC (decision 2/CP.3) were the values of "<a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a>" calculated for the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Second_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Second Assessment Report">IPCC Second Assessment Report</a>. These figures are used for converting the various greenhouse gas emissions into comparable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co2e" title="Co2e">carbon dioxide equivalents</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>-eq) when computing overall sources and sinks. Source: <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://unfccc.int/resource/docs/cop3/07a01.pdf#page=31" rel="nofollow">"Methodological issues related to the Kyoto protocol"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Report of the Conference of the Parties on its third session, held at Kyoto from 1 to 11 December 1997, United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change. 25 March 1998<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 February</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Methodological+issues+related+to+the+Kyoto+protocol&amp;rft.pub=Report+of+the+Conference+of+the+Parties+on+its+third+session%2C+held+at+Kyoto+from+1+to+11+December+1997%2C+United+Nations+Framework+Convention+on+Climate+Change&amp;rft.date=1998-03-25&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Funfccc.int%2Fresource%2Fdocs%2Fcop3%2F07a01.pdf%23page%3D31&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKyoto+Protocol"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-unfccc1997-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-unfccc1997_32-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<cite class="citation pressrelease"><a class="external text" href="http://unfccc.int/cop3/fccc/info/indust.htm" rel="nofollow">"Industrialized countries to cut greenhouse gas emissions by 5.2%"</a> (Press release). <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a>. 11 December 1997<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Emissions data given in this table may not fully reflect progress towards the first-round Kyoto targets. This is because of the implicit trading of targets under the EU ETS (see <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Intergovernmental_Emissions_Trading" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol#Intergovernmental Emissions Trading</a>), possible effects of trade in AAUs (see <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Trade_in_AAUs" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol#Trade in AAUs</a>), and the use of different emissions base years for some Parties (see <a href='/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#2012_emission_targets_and_"flexible_mechanisms"' title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol#2012 emission targets and "flexible mechanisms"</a>).</span>
